# CHAPTER 1 - THE KEY (Medieval Timeline - 1347, Mainz)

## Scene 0: The Scriptorium

### St. Martin's Monastery, Mainz - Dawn

The smell hit Thomas first: blood and ink, mixed with the smoke from overturned candles.

Brother Hamo lay sprawled across the copying desk, his robes dark with spreading crimson. The manuscript he'd been working on—a commentary on Boethius—was ruined, blood soaking through the vellum.

Thomas froze in the scriptorium doorway, his breakfast bread forgotten in his hand.

"Hamo?"

The old monk's hand twitched. Thomas rushed forward and knelt beside him.

"Thomas." Hamo's voice was wet, bubbling. A narrow, precise wound gaped in his chest. The dagger was gone. "Took... too long..."

"I'll get the infirmarian—"

"No." Hamo's grip was surprisingly strong on Thomas's wrist. "No time. They're coming. The Gray Robes."

"Who—"

"The Order." Hamo coughed, blood on his lips. His other hand fumbled in his robes, pulled out something small. Iron. "Your grandfather... Wilhelm... he made me promise. If anything happened... give this to you."

He pressed the object into Thomas's palm. A key. Crude iron, with an eye symbol etched into the handle.

Thomas stared at it. "I don't understand—"

"The cipher." Hamo's eyes were losing focus. "The marks in the margins. Wilhelm's network. This is the... only tool. The only one that can read it." His grip tightened. "Hide it. Never let the Order—"

Footsteps in the corridor. Multiple sets. Heavy boots, not sandals.

Hamo's expression changed. Terror. "Go. Now. The tunnels beneath the wine cellar. Go!"

"I'm not leaving you—"

"You're weak, Thomas. Everyone knows it. Weak at digging, weak at hauling, weak at everything that matters." Hamo smiled, sad and certain. "But Wilhelm saw something in you. Some gift. And if he believed it..." Blood ran from the corner of his mouth. "Then I believe it too."

The footsteps were closer. Voices—calm, methodical, asking questions in the chapter house.

"Go," Hamo whispered. "See the patterns your grandfather saw. Make it matter. Make my death—"

His eyes went distant. His hand went slack.

Thomas knelt there, frozen, the iron key cutting into his palm.

The voices were in the cloister now. Getting closer.

"—the scribe boy, Thomas of Eltville. Where is he?"

"—Brother Hamo was the last to see the manuscript—"

"—search the scriptorium first—"

Thomas looked at Hamo's body. At the ruined Boethius. At the key in his hand.

Grandfather Wilhelm had died for this. Now Hamo.

*Weak. Useless. Can't even protect the people who believe in you.*

The footsteps turned toward the scriptorium.

Thomas ran.

He knew these corridors—had scrubbed them often enough during penance. The wine cellar. Left at the refectory, down the stone stairs, past the chapter house. He could hear shouts behind him now, no longer bothering with stealth.

The wine cellar was dark, cool, smelling of earth and fermentation. Thomas squeezed between the large barrels, found the tunnel entrance Hamo had mentioned—a low arch behind the oldest cask, barely visible in the shadows.

He dropped to his knees, crawled into darkness.

Behind him, the cellar door crashed open.

"—saw him come this way—"

"—check behind the barrels—"

Thomas crawled faster, hands scraping stone, the key clutched tight enough to draw blood. The tunnel sloped down, turned, opened into a larger passage. Old Roman construction, forgotten when the monastery was built centuries ago.

He could hear them behind him, but the tunnel was too narrow for a grown man in armor.

"Get the boy from the village. Someone who can fit."

Thomas didn't wait to hear more. He ran through the darkness, one hand on the tunnel wall, until he saw light ahead—a grating that opened onto the city streets.

He pushed through, emerged in an alley stinking of offal and tanning chemicals.

Free—for now.

Thomas looked at the key in his hand. Blood on it—his own, from the sharp edges. Or Hamo's, from those final moments.

*Your grandfather saw something in you. Hamo saw it too.*

*And they both died for it.*

Thomas had never felt less worthy of anyone's faith.

But he was alive. And the Gray Robes wanted this key badly enough to kill for it.

*Then I'd better figure out why.*

He pulled his hood up and stepped into the crowded streets of Mainz, where the plague bells were ringing and the world was dying.

And somewhere in this chaos, there had to be answers.

---

## Scene 1: Wilhelm's Lesson

Thomas's lungs burned as he ran through the twisted streets of Mainz. The key pressed into his palm, its edges cutting deeper.

*Grandfather.*

The memory came hard and fast: eight years old, standing in his father's vineyard while his grandfather showed him a manuscript.

"Do you see the marks, Thomas?" Wilhelm's weathered hands had pointed to tiny dots in the margin, his silver ring catching the light as he moved. "Not decoration. Information. Each position means something. Each color carries weight."

"Why hide it, Grandfather?"

"Because power wants to stay powerful. And the most powerful thing in this world isn't gold or swords. It's knowing what others don't." Wilhelm had smiled sadly. "The Church says knowledge should serve God. I say knowledge should serve humanity. But there are those who say knowledge should serve only the worthy."

"Are we worthy?"

"That's the question, isn't it?" Wilhelm had closed the book. "Your father thinks I'm a fool. He wants you in the vineyards, learning useful work. But I think you have your grandmother's eyes—you see patterns. And that's worth more than strong arms."

Three weeks later, Wilhelm was dead. "Fell from his horse," they said. But Thomas remembered the stranger who'd visited the day before—the silver key pendant at his throat. The same symbol now etched on the iron key in Thomas's hand.

*They killed him. They killed Grandfather for this.*

Thomas stumbled into an alley, pressed his back against the cold stone wall. His father's voice echoed: *"Weak. Useless. Can't even dig a proper hole without whining."*

Maybe he was weak. Maybe he was useless. But Grandfather had believed he could see patterns.

He looked at the key. At the eye symbol staring back.

*Then I'd better start seeing them.*

---

## Scene 2: First Glimpse of Margarethe

The cloth merchant's quarter was a maze of drying racks and funeral shrouds. Thomas moved through it like a ghost, his monk's robes stained with tunnel filth.

A woman worked at a sorting table, her hands moving with numb precision through piles of clothing. Plague-stained linens. The clothes of the dead.

Thomas had seen enough death in the scriptorium. He started to turn away.

"You're bleeding," the woman said without looking up.

Thomas looked at his palm. The key had cut deeper than he'd thought. "It's nothing."

"Infection kills as surely as plague." She finally looked up.

She was younger than he'd expected—mid-twenties, perhaps. Her face was sharp, intelligent, a scar running along her jawline. Her hands, though working with fabric, were callused in ways that spoke of harder work than cloth.

"The Gray Robes are watching the gates," she said, returning to her work. "Whatever you stole, they want it back badly. Three men just went through here asking about a scribe boy. I told them I saw you heading toward the cathedral."

"Why would you—"

"Because the Order burned my father for having books they didn't approve of." Her voice was flat. "I don't help them. Ever."

Thomas stepped closer. "The books. Did they have... marks? Patterns in the margins?"

Her hands stilled. For just a moment, her mask cracked. "Who are you?"

"Thomas. Of Eltville."

"Eltville." She tested the name. "Wilhelm's grandson?"

His breath caught. "You knew him?"

"My father knew him. Worked with him." She studied Thomas with new intensity. "You look like him. Same soft hands. Same naive eyes that think the world should be fair."

"I don't think—"

"Good. Because it's not." She pulled a strip of cloth from her pile, walked around the table. "Give me your hand."

Thomas hesitated, then extended his bleeding palm.

She bound the wound with efficient, practiced movements. This close, he could smell her—rosemary, lavender, and something acrid. Chemicals used in cloth dyeing. Or erasing ink.

"The river path," she said, tying off the bandage. "Go before dark. There's a warehouse district. Third building, red mark on the door. Knock twice. If I'm not there by morning, run south and don't look back."

"I don't even know your name."

"Margarethe." She met his eyes directly. "And if you're carrying what I think you're carrying, Thomas of Eltville, knowing my name might be the most dangerous thing you ever learn."

She turned back to her work, dismissing him.

Thomas walked toward the river, his bandaged hand throbbing. He looked back once.

Margarethe was watching him. When their eyes met, she didn't look away.

For reasons he couldn't name, his chest tightened.

Then he ran.

---

## Scene 3: Margarethe's Backstory

Margarethe's hands shook as she returned to sorting the linens.

*Wilhelm's grandson.*

She closed her eyes, and memory—unwanted, uninvited—came anyway:

**Seven years ago. Age nineteen.**

Her father's warehouse. The smell of imported silks and hidden manuscripts. Brother Anselm standing over Heinrich's broken body, his soft voice asking questions, his calm hands wielding instruments of precision and pain.

"Where is the index, Heinrich? Where does Wilhelm keep the master list?"

"There is no... master list..." Her father's voice, barely recognizable through blood and broken teeth.

"We know about the network. The cipher maps. The locations hidden across Christendom. Wilhelm is dead, but his knowledge remains. Tell me where."

"Knowledge... should be free..."

"Knowledge in the wrong hands is a plague worse than the Death outside these walls." Anselm had sounded genuinely sad. "I take no pleasure in this, old friend. But the Order cannot allow chaos. Tell me, and I will make it quick."

Margarethe, hidden in the loft, had bitten through her lip to keep from screaming.

Her father had looked up—directly at her hiding place. Their eyes met.

*Don't speak. Don't move. Live.*

Then: "I'll tell you. The index is in... Abbey of Fulda. Behind the altar stone. Third—"

Anselm's dagger had been quick. Merciful, even.

The lie had saved her life. There was no index at Fulda. The real locations were in her father's head—and he'd spent her entire childhood teaching her to memorize them.

*"In case something happens to me, little raven. In case the Order comes. You must be the library they cannot burn."*

She'd become exactly that. Two hundred and seventeen locations, memorized perfectly. Monasteries, private collections, merchant safe houses. The complete network of the Aethelred Cipher's hidden knowledge.

She was the index. The living catalog.

And she'd just revealed herself to Wilhelm's grandson.

*Stupid. Dangerous. Weak.*

But when she'd seen Thomas's eyes—that same naive belief that the world could be better, that knowledge mattered more than survival—she'd seen her father. And Wilhelm. And all the good men the Order had murdered.

Maybe one more wouldn't matter.

Or maybe this time, she'd be fast enough to save one.

She finished sorting the linens, locked her shop, and began preparing for the evening.

If Wilhelm's grandson was carrying the cipher key—and she was certain he was—the Order wouldn't stop at gates and guards. They'd hunt him properly.

And Thomas of Eltville, soft-handed scribe who probably couldn't throw a punch to save his life, would be dead by nightfall unless someone kept him alive.

Margarethe strapped her daggers beneath her cloak. The good ones, perfectly balanced. Her father's blades.

She hated getting attached.

But some debts ran deeper than survival.

---

## Scene 4: The Rescue

### Night. River Path.

Thomas followed the path along the Rhine, the water black and cold beside him. Behind him, the city bells tolled for the dead. Another plague cart rattling through the streets.

He'd made it maybe half a mile when he heard the footsteps.

Two sets. Heavy boots on cobblestones. Not bothering to hide.

Thomas's heart hammered. He walked faster. The footsteps matched his pace.

"Scribe boy!" A voice called behind him. "Brother Anselm would speak with you. No need to run."

Thomas ran.

The river path narrowed. Warehouses loomed on one side, the drop to the water on the other. Thomas's sandals slipped on wet stone. His breath came in ragged gasps.

*Weak. Useless. Can't even run properly.*

A hand grabbed his shoulder, yanked him back. Thomas spun, fell, hit the cobblestones hard. Stars exploded across his vision.

"Easy now," the first man said. He wore the gray robes, the silver key pendant. "We just want the little iron toy you're carrying. Give it over, and maybe you live to copy another psalm."

The second man drew a dagger. "Or don't, and save us the trouble."

Thomas's hand closed around the key in his pocket. Hamo's blood was probably still on it. Grandfather's legacy.

"No," he whispered.

"What was that?"

"I said no." Louder now. "You killed my grandfather. You killed Hamo. You're not getting this."

The first man sighed. "Brave and stupid. The worst combination." He nodded to his partner. "Make it quick."

The dagger came toward Thomas's throat.

And stopped.

The second man made a strange gurgling sound. He looked down at the blade protruding from his neck, confused. Then he collapsed.

The first man spun, reaching for his own weapon—

Margarethe rose from the shadows behind him, a second dagger already in motion. She drove it under his ribs with practiced precision, twisted, withdrew. The man fell.

Thomas stared from his position on the ground. His ears rang. The world narrowed to Margarethe standing over the bodies, wiping her blades on the first man's cloak.

She looked at Thomas. Her expression was calm. Practiced.

"Breathe," she said. "Unless you want to faint and crack your skull. Then by all means."

Thomas breathed. The ringing subsided.

Margarethe sheathed her daggers, then extended a hand. Thomas took it. Her grip was strong, callused. She pulled him to his feet.

"You were supposed to go to the warehouse," she said.

"I was going—"

"Too slow. The Order doesn't just guard gates. They watch the paths too." She looked at the bodies without emotion. "I followed you. Figured you'd be dead within the hour if I didn't."

"You've been following me since—"

"Since you left my shop. Yes." She checked the alley behind them. "These two won't be the last. Can you run?"

"Yes."

"Then run. Toward the warehouses. Third door, red mark. I'll make sure no one else is tracking you, then meet you there."

"Wait—" Thomas found his voice. "You killed them."

"Yes."

"You just... killed two men."

"Would you prefer I let them kill you?" Her tone was matter-of-fact, but Thomas saw something flicker in her eyes. "This isn't the scriptorium, Thomas. Out here, you either run or you bleed. Sometimes both. But at least you get to choose."

She turned to leave.

"Thank you," Thomas said.

Margarethe paused. Didn't turn around. "Don't thank me yet. You're not safe. Neither of us is." A beat. "But I owed your grandfather a debt. I pay my debts."

"Is that the only reason you helped me?"

Now she did turn. Looked at him directly. "I don't know. Ask me again when we're not standing next to corpses."

Then she disappeared into the shadows.

Thomas ran for the warehouse. His hands shook—from fear, adrenaline, or the memory of her touch, he couldn't say.

---

## Scene 5: The Warehouse Interrogation

Thomas found the warehouse. Third building. Red mark painted on the door. He knocked twice.

Silence.

He waited. Minutes dragged. Every shadow became a gray cloak.

Finally, the door opened. Margarethe stood there, still wearing the same bloodstained dress. She'd made no effort to clean it.

"Inside. Quick."

The warehouse was vast, dark, and cold. Bales of fabric rose like monuments in the gloom. A single lantern burned on a makeshift table.

Margarethe locked the door behind him. Three separate bolts. Then she turned, arms crossed, and studied him.

"Sit."

Thomas sat on a crate. His legs were grateful for it—they'd been shaking since the river path.

Margarethe didn't sit. She circled him slowly, like a merchant appraising wares.

"You're a terrible liar," she said finally.

"I didn't lie—"

"You're afraid. Of me, of the Order, of yourself. That's honest." She stopped in front of him. "But you're hiding something. Everyone does. So here's how this works: I ask questions, you answer truthfully, and maybe we both survive the week. Lie to me, and I throw you out. Clear?"

Thomas nodded.

"Name."

"Thomas. Thomas of Eltville."

"Family."

"My father is alive. In Eltville. We... don't speak. He wanted me in the vineyards. I was too weak for it." The admission hurt. "My grandfather raised me. Wilhelm. He died when I was eight."

"How did he die?"

Thomas met her eyes. "The Order killed him. I didn't know it then. I thought it was an accident. But I saw the man who visited the day before. He wore a silver key."

Something softened in Margarethe's expression. Not much. But enough.

"Your grandfather was brilliant," she said quietly. "Reckless, but brilliant. My father said Wilhelm could hold an entire library in his head. Memorize every location, every cipher mark, every hidden text." She paused. "Did he teach you?"

"Some. He showed me how to see patterns. How to remember sequences. But I was young. I didn't understand..." Thomas pulled out the iron key. "Until Hamo gave me this."

Margarethe's breath caught. She reached for the key, then stopped herself. "May I?"

"Yes."

She took it gently, reverently. Turned it over in her hands, examining the etched eye symbol.

"I thought this was lost," she whispered. "When my father died, Anselm searched for weeks. He tore apart our warehouse, our home. He never found it."

"Because your father had it?"

"My father. He kept it separate. Safe." She looked up at Thomas. "Do you know what this is?"

"Hamo said it reads the cipher. Shows which dots matter."

"It's more than that." Margarethe pulled out a folded piece of parchment from her bodice. She spread it on the table.

Thomas leaned forward. The parchment showed what looked like a manuscript page, covered in dense Latin text. But in the margins, faintly visible, were geometric dot patterns.

Margarethe placed the iron key over the page. The eye symbol aligned with a specific section of text. Through the aperture, certain dots were highlighted.

"The key is a template," she explained. "Position it correctly, and it tells you which dots to count, which to ignore. Without it, the cipher looks random. With it..." She counted quickly. "Abbey of St. Gall. Third shelf. Volume on Aristotelian comedy."

Thomas stared. "That's impossible. Those dots—they're tiny. Random—"

"Not random. Never random. Every mark has meaning. Position, color, depth. It's a language, Thomas. A mathematical language your grandfather helped create." She handed the key back. "And now you're holding the only decoding tool that exists."

"Why not make more?"

"Because more copies mean more chances for the Order to find one. This key is unique. The knowledge of how to make it died with the original architects." Margarethe's jaw tightened. "My father tried to recreate it. He couldn't. The mathematics were beyond him. Beyond anyone alive."

Thomas turned the key over in his hands. Such a small thing. Iron, worthless. But it held the power to unlock centuries of hidden knowledge.

"What do we do with it?" he asked.

"We?" Margarethe raised an eyebrow.

"You saved my life. You clearly care about protecting this cipher. I have the key, but I don't know how to use it properly. You know the system, but you don't have the tool." Thomas met her gaze. "Separately, we're incomplete. Together..."

"Together we're bigger targets."

"Maybe. Or maybe we're strong enough to actually do something with this."

Margarethe studied him for a long moment. "You're naive."

"Yes."

"Weak. Probably useless in a fight."

"Almost certainly."

"And you think because we share a dead grandfather's legacy, that makes us allies?"

"I think," Thomas said carefully, "that you could have let those men kill me. You could have taken the key off my corpse and been done with it. But you didn't. So either you're a worse pragmatist than you pretend, or..." He trailed off.

"Or?"

"Or you're just as tired of being alone as I am."

The silence stretched between them. The lantern flickered.

Finally, Margarethe sat on a crate across from him. "Here's what you need to understand, Thomas of Eltville. Everyone I protect dies. My mother. My father. The merchants who worked with him. The Order doesn't leave loose ends."

"But you're still alive."

"Because I'm careful. Because I trust no one. Because I've made myself useful enough to too many people that killing me would cause problems." She leaned forward. "You want to be allies? Fine. But don't mistake alliance for friendship. Don't expect me to save you again. And don't—" Her voice caught slightly. "Don't ask me to care whether you live or die. Because everyone I care about ends up dead."

Thomas should have been intimidated. Instead, he heard what she tried to bury: grief, and something close to fear.

"I won't," he said softly. "I'll try not to get killed, anyway. Can't promise about the caring part."

"What?"

"I already care whether you live or die. You saved my life. That tends to create attachment."

Margarethe stared at him. Then, unexpectedly, she laughed—sharp and bitter. "You're going to be a problem, aren't you?"

"Probably."

She shook her head, but something in her expression had shifted—less open, not softer, but no longer entirely sealed.

"We start tomorrow," she said, standing. "I'll teach you to decode the cipher properly. You'll teach me everything your grandfather told you about the network. And we'll figure out what to do with this damned key before the Order finds us."

"Where do I sleep?"

"There." She pointed to a pile of wool blankets in the corner. "I'll be on the other side of the warehouse. If you try anything, I have more daggers."

"I wouldn't—"

"I know. You're too naive to be dangerous." But she almost smiled. "Sleep, Thomas. Tomorrow we find out if you're really Wilhelm's grandson or just another dead scribe waiting to happen."

She walked into the shadows, leaving Thomas alone with the lantern and the weight of his grandfather's legacy.

He lay on the wool blankets, the key pressed against his chest.

For the first time since Hamo's death, he felt something other than fear.

He felt purpose.

And, though he didn't quite understand it yet, he felt the first stirrings of something else entirely when he thought about the scarred woman with daggers and grief.

But that was a problem for tomorrow.

Tonight, he just needed to survive.
# CHAPTER 2 - THE NETWORK (Medieval Timeline - 1347, Mainz)

## Scene 1: Morning After - The Lesson Begins

### Warehouse, Dawn

Thomas woke to the sound of steel on stone.

The scrape of whetstone against blade cut through his dreams—rhythmic, deliberate, patient. He opened his eyes to gray dawn filtering through gaps in the warehouse walls. Dust motes swirled in the pale shafts of light like ash from distant fires.

The air smelled of damp wool and old fabric, cold enough that his breath misted. His back ached where the stone floor had pressed through the blankets all night. His ribs protested when he sat up—bruised from yesterday's fall on the cobblestones when the Gray Robes had cornered him.

When Margarethe had saved him.

She sat cross-legged on a crate fifteen feet away, methodically sharpening her daggers. She hadn't looked at him yet. The sound of the whetstone was almost meditative—scrape, pause, test, repeat. How many blades did she carry? Thomas had counted four yesterday. There might be more.

"You snore," Margarethe said without looking up.

Thomas blinked, still foggy with sleep. "I don't—" He stopped. "Do I?"

"Like a dying goat." She tested the blade's edge against her thumb with the casual confidence of someone who'd done it a thousand times. Nodded in satisfaction. Set it aside, started on the next. "But quietly enough. No one heard us from the street."

"How long have you been awake?" Thomas stood, his joints popping. His mouth tasted like copper and old fear.

"Since before dawn. Habit." She finally looked at him. Her eyes were sharp, assessing—the same way she'd looked at him yesterday in her cloth shop. Weighing his value. Measuring his weaknesses. "You slept well for someone who watched two men die."

The river-path images surged back: a throat opening under Margarethe's blade, lanternlight turning blood black, the wet gurgle that went on too long. The second man folding to the stones, her dagger still in his neck.

Thomas's stomach rolled.

"I didn't have nightmares," he said quietly. "Should I have?"

"Most people do. First time they see death up close." She sheathed the first dagger, started on the third. "Either you're tougher than you look, or you're very good at lying to yourself."

Thomas thought about it. "Maybe both."

She almost smiled. "Maybe."

He walked to the makeshift table—a rough plank balanced on two crates. Someone, probably Margarethe, had left bread and cheese there. The bread was hard as stone, the cheese not much softer. He tore off pieces of each, chewed mechanically. It tasted like dust and salt. His stomach accepted it grudgingly.

"We start today," Margarethe said, setting down her whetstone. The sound of scraping stopped, leaving the warehouse suddenly quiet. She stood, walked to a stack of fabric bales against the far wall. Her movements were fluid, economical—no wasted motion. "Your grandfather's gift won't save you if you don't know how to use it."

She pushed the bales aside. Behind them was a wooden chest, old and scarred, bound with iron straps. Margarethe produced a key from somewhere—Thomas didn't see where she'd been hiding it. Maybe her boot. Maybe somewhere else.

She unlocked the chest.

Inside were manuscripts.

Thomas's breath caught. Even from here, he could see the quality—good vellum, careful script, the kind of work that took months to produce. Each one was worth more than his father's entire vineyard.

"My father's collection," Margarethe said softly, and for the first time since they'd met, her voice carried something other than bitter practicality. Something like grief. "The ones he hid before the Order came. Not the originals—those are scattered across Christendom in libraries and private collections. But copies. Careful copies." She pulled out three, carried them to the table with reverence. "Everything I know about the cipher, I learned from these."

Thomas leaned over them. The vellum was beautiful—smooth, nearly translucent in the dawn light. The script was precise, probably done by a professional scribe. Latin text: psalms, biblical commentary, what looked like a merchant's ledger. And in the margins, faint but deliberate, geometric patterns of colored dots.

Red. Blue. Yellow.

Just like the ones he'd seen in Hamo's scriptorium before the blood had ruined everything.

"Sit," Margarethe commanded.

Thomas sat.

She pulled the iron key from Thomas's pocket—he hadn't even felt her take it. Her fingers were quick, practiced. A pickpocket's touch. She placed the key on the table between them. The eye symbol etched into the handle caught the light, seemed to stare at both of them. Accusing. Demanding.

"The cipher is a language," Margarethe began. Her teacher's voice was different from her merchant voice or her survivor voice—more patient, almost kind. "But not one written in words. It's written in positions, colors, and counts." She pointed to the first manuscript—a psalm. "Look at this page. What do you see?"

Thomas studied it. Beautiful Latin text, each letter formed with care. *Dominus regit me...* The Lord is my shepherd. And in the margin, near Psalm 23, a cluster of dots. Three red, two blue, one yellow. Arranged in what looked like a triangular pattern.

"Dots," he said.

"Deeper. What pattern do you see?"

Thomas looked closer. Squinted. The dots weren't random placement. The three red ones formed a perfect triangle, apex pointing upward. The two blue dots sat at the triangle's base, equidistant. The single yellow dot was offset to the right, like a waypoint.

"Geometry," he said slowly, his mind beginning to turn over the problem. "The placement is deliberate. The red dots form a specific shape."

"Good." Margarethe's voice held a note of approval that warmed him more than it should. "Now watch."

She took the iron key and positioned it over the page. Her hands moved with the precision of someone who'd done this hundreds of times. The eye symbol aligned with a specific section of the psalm—the word *"righteous."*

Through the aperture in the key's handle, certain dots were highlighted while others fell into shadow.

"The key is a filter," Margarethe explained. "Position it correctly over the textual anchor, and it tells you which dots to count, which to ignore." She counted quickly under her breath, her lips barely moving. "Three red, skip the blues, one yellow. Three-one. Position indicator."

"Position of what?" Thomas leaned forward, fascinated despite his fear.

"The location being encoded." She pulled out a second manuscript—a merchant's ledger, the pages stained with what might have been wine or blood. Found a page with similar marginal markings. "Now watch. Same key, different alignment."

She positioned the key differently. This time, the aperture highlighted only the blue dots, leaving the others in shadow.

"Two blues, skip the yellow, zero reds. Two-zero. That's a danger marker. A warning." She met his eyes. "The cipher isn't just hiding information—it's protecting people. Warning them where not to go."

Thomas's mind raced, patterns clicking into place like the pins in a lock finding their positions. "So the colors mean different things. Red is safe locations. Blue is dangerous. Yellow is—"

"Neutral ground. Trade routes, markets, places where the network operates but doesn't control." Margarethe set the key down between them. "The cipher encodes three types of information: location, status, and timing. Together, they create a map of the known world that tells you where to hide, when to travel, and who to trust."

"Timing?" Thomas asked.

She pointed to a cluster of yellow dots on another page, arranged in a pattern that looked almost like a clock face—if clocks had twelve positions instead of numbers. "Seasonal markers. When to travel, when to hide, when to wait. The network moves with the seasons, with trade fairs, with pilgrimages. Your grandfather was brilliant at reading these. He could look at a page and memorize fifty locations in an hour."

Thomas felt the weight of that legacy settle on his shoulders. "I'm not my grandfather."

"No." Margarethe's gaze was steady, unflinching. "But you have his eyes. The way you look at things—seeing patterns, seeing connections. That's his gift." She pushed the manuscripts toward him. "Your turn. Take the key. Find me a location."

Thomas picked up the iron key. It was heavier than it looked, the metal cold and rough against his palm. The edges were sharp enough to cut if you held it wrong. He positioned it over the first manuscript, trying to mimic Margarethe's smooth movements.

The aperture didn't align properly. Through it, the dots looked random, meaningless.

He adjusted, moving the key slightly left. Still wrong.

"Not like that," Margarethe said, her voice patient. "The key aligns with specific words, not just anywhere on the page. Look for repeated phrases—textual anchors. The cipher-makers used common biblical language so the pattern would work across multiple manuscripts."

Thomas scanned the psalm. *"The Lord is my shepherd..."* Too common—that phrase appeared in hundreds of texts. But further down: *"He restoreth my soul."* Less common. More specific.

He aligned the eye symbol with the word *"restoreth."*

Through the aperture, three red dots and one yellow appeared in sharp focus.

"Three-one," he breathed. His heart hammered with sudden excitement. "That's... Abbey of St. Gall?"

Margarethe's eyebrows rose. "How did you—"

"You said it yesterday. When you demonstrated with the sample page." The memory was crystal clear in Thomas's mind, as if the words were written in light. "Three-one was the St. Gall location. Third shelf, first volume. Commentary on Aristotelian comedy."

She stared at him. "You memorized that from one demonstration?"

"I... yes?" Thomas looked at the key in his hand, then back at her. "Is that unusual?"

For the first time since they'd met, Margarethe smiled—a real smile, not the bitter edge she usually wore. It transformed her face, softened the scar on her jaw, made her look younger. Almost happy.

"You really are Wilhelm's grandson," she said quietly.

Thomas felt warmth bloom in his chest that had nothing to do with the cipher or the manuscripts or the danger they were in. Something about the way she said it—like she'd been hoping it was true and was relieved to have it confirmed.

"Again," Margarethe said, pulling out another manuscript. Her merchant mask was back, but something had shifted. "Find me another location."

Thomas worked through it. This one was harder—a dense commentary on Boethius's *Consolation of Philosophy*, the Latin theological and philosophical, full of complex conditional clauses. But the pattern was there, waiting to be found. He scanned for repeated phrases, found the textual anchor (*"fortune's wheel"*), aligned the key carefully, counted the dots that came into focus.

"Two red, three yellow. Position two-three. That's..." He frowned, searching his memory. "I don't know that one."

"Cologne. Private collection, merchant district. Second house, third door." Margarethe took the key back, and her fingers brushed his. "You're learning fast. Too fast, maybe."

"Is that a problem?"

"It is if you start thinking you're invincible." She closed the manuscripts with a finality that said the lesson was over. "The cipher is powerful, Thomas. It can tell you where to hide, who to trust, how to survive. But it can't tell you everything. It can't warn you about betrayal from someone who seems trustworthy. It can't predict when someone you consider a friend will sell you to the Order for fifty gold marks and a promise of safety."

"Oskar wouldn't—" Thomas started.

"Anyone would. For the right price. Or the right threat." She stood, walked to the warehouse door, checked the street through a crack in the weathered boards. Morning light slanted in, harsh and revealing. "Which is why we're going to the market. You need to see how the network really works. Not on vellum and in theory, but in flesh and blood and lies."

Thomas stood, brushing breadcrumbs from his robe. "You're taking me to meet more people? After you just said anyone might betray us?"

"No. I'm taking you to watch people. There's a difference." She turned, and her expression was serious, almost grave. "The cipher is information. But information without context is just noise. You need to learn to read people the way you read manuscripts. Their tells, their patterns, their weaknesses."

"I'm not good at that," Thomas admitted.

"I know. You're transparent as glass." She pulled a cloak from a hook on the wall, tossed it to him. It smelled like rosemary and old smoke—like her. "Which is why you're going to keep your mouth shut and watch. Understand?"

Thomas caught the cloak, pulled it around his shoulders. "Understood."

"Good." Margarethe pulled her own cloak on, checked that her daggers were concealed beneath the fabric. "And Thomas?"

"Yes?"

"If something happens—if we're separated, if the Order finds us—you run. You don't try to be brave. You don't try to save me. You just run and don't look back."

He wanted to argue. Wanted to say he wouldn't abandon her the way everyone in his life had abandoned him. The way his father had sent him to the monastery. The way the monks had looked through him like he was invisible. The way Hamo had died before Thomas could save him.

But the look in her eyes stopped him.

"I'll run," he lied.

She studied him for a long moment, and he saw the exact instant she knew he was lying. But instead of calling him on it, she just sighed. "You're a terrible liar. But I appreciate the effort."

Then she opened the door, and they stepped into the plague-haunted streets of Mainz.

---

## Scene 2: The Market - Gathering Intelligence

The market square was a ghost of what it must have been.

Thomas had seen it before the plague—had walked through it with Hamo once, two years ago, when the stalls had overflowed with goods and the crowd had been so thick you could barely move. Bright fabrics, fresh bread, the smell of roasting meat and spiced wine. Laughter. Children running between carts. Life.

Now it was dying.

Half the stalls were empty, their owners dead or fled. The merchants who remained sold food at prices that would have seemed criminal a year ago—turnips for a silver penny, half a loaf of bread for three. Clothing stripped from plague houses, the blood stains scrubbed out but the death still clinging to the fabric. Amulets and charms that promised protection, carved with saints' names or stranger symbols.

The smell was overwhelming. Rotting vegetables mixed with unwashed bodies. The sharp tang of vinegar—people dousing themselves with it, hoping it would ward off the plague. And underneath it all, seeping up from the gutters and the cellars and the mass graves beyond the city walls, the sweet-sick stench of death.

Thomas pulled his hood lower, trying not to breathe too deeply. The cloth over his face helped, but not enough.

Margarethe moved through the crowd with practiced ease. She'd transformed again—not the hardened survivor from the warehouse, not the patient teacher. Now she was a cloth merchant, shrewd and focused, examining goods with a critical eye. She'd wrapped a scarf around her lower face—plague caution, but also disguise. The scar on her jaw was hidden.

Thomas followed three steps behind, as she'd instructed.

"Third stall on the left," she murmured without turning her head. Her voice was so low he barely heard it over the market noise. "Bookseller. Gray beard, green tunic. Watch him. Don't stare, but watch."

Thomas looked.

The bookseller was old—seventy at least, maybe older. His beard was more white than gray, his face weathered like old leather. He sat behind a small stall displaying religious texts and prayer books. Nothing remarkable. Just another old man trying to survive by selling salvation to the desperate.

But then Thomas noticed the pattern.

People didn't browse the bookseller's stall casually. They came with purpose. A man in merchant's clothes would approach, lean close, whisper something. The bookseller would nod, pull a specific book from his stock, open it to a specific page. The merchant would look, nod back, and leave. No money changed hands. No book was purchased.

A woman in widow's black. Same pattern. Whisper, specific book, specific page, nod, leave.

A younger man, nervously glancing around. Whisper, book, page, and this time the man's face went pale before he hurried away.

"Information trading," Margarethe said quietly, her voice barely audible. "Oskar doesn't sell books. He sells what's in them."

Thomas watched as another woman approached—middle-aged, well-dressed, probably a merchant's wife. She whispered something urgent. The bookseller—Oskar—pulled a psalter, showed her a page. Thomas couldn't see the page from here, but he knew what would be in the margins. Colored dots. The cipher.

The woman's face went white. She nodded once, quickly, and walked away fast enough to be running without actually running.

"What did he tell her?" Thomas asked, keeping his voice as low as Margarethe's.

"Probably that her husband's road is no longer safe. Or that the plague has reached the next town. Or that the Order is asking after their name." Margarethe angled them closer, stopping at a neighboring stall that sold fabric dyes. "The network lives on news. Oskar is a knot in their web. He knows who's traveling, who's died, where the Order is searching, which roads are safe, which cities are sealed. People pay him in gossip and coin. He pays them back in survival."

She picked up a small vial of indigo dye, examining it with apparent interest while watching Oskar from the corner of her eye. Thomas mimicked her, pretending to study the pigments.

"Why don't we just ask him what we need to know?" Thomas whispered.

"Because asking costs more than observing. Information freely given is worthless—he'd wonder why we didn't already know. And because—" She went rigid. "Don't turn around. Gray Robe. Two stalls behind us, near the vegetable seller. He's watching the bookseller."

Thomas's heart hammered. He forced himself to stay still, to keep examining a jar of dye like it was the most fascinating thing in the world. The pungent smell of woad and madder root filled his nose.

"Is he looking at us?" Thomas breathed, barely moving his lips.

"No. He's watching Oskar. The Order knows he's a network contact. They're watching who speaks to him, trying to name the rest of the web." Margarethe's voice was tight, controlled. "We need to approach, but carefully."

"Then we can't—"

"We can. But we have to be smarter about it." She purchased the small vial of dye—unnecessary, but maintaining her merchant cover. Paid in small copper coins that she counted out slowly, making conversation about the weather and the plague like any normal customer. Then she moved away from the stall. "Follow. Not directly. Circle around. Act like we're shopping."

Thomas obeyed. They moved through the market like water finding a path, never stopping in one place too long, never drawing attention. Margarethe paused at a vegetable stall, haggled over withered turnips that probably cost ten times what they should. Moved to a rack of used clothing, examined a cloak with holes in it. All the while, she was angling them toward Oskar's stall from a different direction, keeping crowds between them and the watching Gray Robe.

Finally, she stopped at a stall selling candles—tallow, cheap, smoking. From here, they had a clear line of sight to Oskar, but the Gray Robe was blocked by a group of women haggling over fish.

"Now," Margarethe said. "Quick and quiet."

They crossed to Oskar's stall. Up close, the bookseller was even older than Thomas had thought. His hands were ink-stained, the fingers bent with age or arthritis. But his eyes were sharp behind wrinkled lids. Intelligent. Watchful.

He looked at Margarethe and went completely still.

"Heinrich's daughter," he said softly. His voice was rough, like he didn't use it much. "I thought you'd fled the city. People said you left after... after your father."

"I tried." Margarethe leaned over a displayed gospel, feigning interest, keeping her voice low. "Got pulled back. Unfinished business."

"The dangerous kind of business, I imagine." But his eyes were kind. Sad. "Heinrich was a good man. I'm sorry for what they did to him."

"So am I." Margarethe turned a page of the gospel, casual. "I need information, Oskar."

"Information always costs." He glanced toward where the Gray Robe was still watching, though his view was obscured by the crowd. "Though for Heinrich's memory, perhaps we can negotiate a discount."

"The Order is hunting someone. Wilhelm of Eltville's grandson." She turned another page. "What's the bounty?"

Oskar's expression didn't change, but his voice dropped even lower. "Fifty gold marks. Alive, preferably. Dead if necessary. They want what he's carrying more than they want him breathing."

Thomas felt cold despite his cloak. Fifty gold marks was more money than most people in Mainz saw in a lifetime.

"Who's hunting?" Margarethe asked.

"Everyone." Oskar glanced again toward the Gray Robe. "Fifty marks is a year's income for a skilled craftsman. Two years for a laborer. The Order has made it clear—turn in the boy, no questions asked, no consequences for whatever else you might have done. Half the city is looking. The other half is staying quiet and hoping he gets away."

"And which half are you?"

"The half that remembers Wilhelm was a good man who deserved better than what they gave him." Oskar pulled another book from his stock—a prayer book, small and worn. He showed Margarethe something inside. Thomas couldn't see what. "There's talk of a cathedral search. Tonight. The Order believes Wilhelm hid something there before he died. Documents, maybe. Or another key."

"Did he?" Margarethe's voice was carefully neutral.

"If he did, I don't know where. He kept his secrets close." Oskar looked at Thomas for the first time. Really looked, his sharp eyes taking in every detail. "You have his eyes. The pattern-seeing gift. I heard it ran in the family, but seeing it..." He shook his head. "Try harder, boy. The world needs what you're carrying."

Thomas swallowed. "I'm trying."

"Try harder." Oskar handed the prayer book to Margarethe. "For your troubles. No charge. Consider it a gift in Heinrich's memory."

Margarethe took it, slipped it into her cloak without examining it. Professional. "Thank you, old friend."

"Don't thank me yet. The Order is tightening the circle. If you're still in Mainz tomorrow, you'll be in chains. Or dead." He looked past them, and his expression shifted. "And you're being watched. The Gray Robe is coming this way. Move. Now."

"Run or hide?" Margarethe asked quietly.

"Neither. Disappear." Oskar raised his voice suddenly, addressing the crowd around them. "These lovely merchants were just leaving! No purchase today, alas, but perhaps next market when their prices are more reasonable!"

Margarethe nodded, loud enough for others to hear. Playing her part perfectly. "Your prices are too high, old man. I'll find better elsewhere."

"My prices are fair!" Oskar shot back, equally loud. "It's your purse that's too light!"

They moved away, blending into the crowd. Thomas's pulse thundered in his ears. He wanted desperately to look back, to see if the Gray Robe was following, but Margarethe's earlier instruction held firm in his mind: *Don't draw attention.*

They wove through the market. Past the plague cart loading new bodies—Thomas tried not to look, but caught a glimpse of a small hand, a child's hand, dangling over the edge. Past a preacher standing on an overturned crate, shouting about God's judgment and the need for repentance. Margarethe's hand found Thomas's elbow, guided him left into a narrow space between stalls, then right into an alley that stank of piss and rotting garbage.

Finally, they stopped in a recessed doorway—some abandoned shop, its door boarded over. Margarethe checked the street behind them, her hand on her dagger.

"Did we lose him?" Thomas asked, breathing hard.

"Maybe. Or maybe he knows where we're going and doesn't need to follow." She pulled out the prayer book Oskar had given her. Opened it carefully.

Inside the front cover, written in tiny, cramped script that was barely legible, was a message:

*Cathedral. Tonight. Midnight. Library accessible through chapter house. Code: Psalm 117, verse 1. Brother Marcus is sympathetic. Will let you in. But leave before Lauds or Anselm will know. One hour. No more. —O*

Margarethe read it twice, her lips moving silently as she memorized each word. Then she tore out the page, balled it up, and put it in her mouth. Chewed.

Thomas stared. "You're eating—"

She swallowed. "Information that can't be stolen is information that survives." She closed the book, dropped it in the gutter where it landed in something foul. "We have our window. Midnight. That gives us eight hours to prepare."

"Prepare how?" Thomas asked, though he suspected he knew the answer.

Margarethe's smile was sharp as broken glass. "I'm going to teach you not to be completely useless. Come on."

They disappeared into the warren of Mainz's back streets, leaving the market and its hunters behind.

---

## Scene 3: Margarethe's Training Montage

Back in the warehouse, Margarethe transformed from merchant to teacher.

Not the patient teacher from this morning, explaining the cipher with care and precision. This was a harder kind of teaching. The kind that left bruises.

"First lesson," she said, bolting the door behind them with all three bolts. "Silence."

Thomas blinked. "What?"

"You move like a drunken cow. Every step announces you're coming." She pointed to the far end of the warehouse, where stacks of fabric bales created a makeshift maze in the shadows. "In the cathedral tonight, you need to be a ghost. So walk from here to there without making a sound."

Thomas looked at the distance. Maybe thirty feet. "That's all?"

"Try it."

He walked. Tried to step carefully, to be quiet.

His sandals scuffed against the stone floor—a harsh scraping sound that echoed in the empty space. A crate creaked as he brushed past it. Somewhere in the darkness, a rat squeaked and fled, its claws scratching on stone.

Margarethe shook her head. "Again. Slower this time. Think about each step."

Thomas tried. Concentrated on placing each foot carefully, shifting his weight gradually.

His robe caught on a protruding nail from one of the crates, ripped with a loud *tear* that made him flinch.

"I'm not built for this," Thomas said, frustration rising. The familiar litany from his father echoing in his head: *Weak. Useless. Can't even do simple work without complaining.*

"No one is built for this. It's a skill, not a talent." Margarethe walked the same path—silent, fluid, barely disturbing the air. She moved like water finding channels, like smoke drifting. "Watch my feet. I step heel-to-toe when I need to be quiet, toe-to-heel when I need speed. I test my weight before committing fully to each step. I know where every creak is, every loose board, every spot that makes noise."

She demonstrated again, this time even slower so he could see every movement. Thomas watched, trying to memorize the pattern. The placement of her feet. The way she shifted her weight. The deliberate care.

"Your turn."

He tried. Failed. The floor creaked under his weight.

Tried again. His robe swished too loudly.

An hour passed. Thomas's frustration mounted with each failure. He was *terrible* at this. Weak at hauling stone, weak at digging, weak at moving silently—weak at everything that mattered in the real world outside of scriptorium walls.

"Stop," Margarethe said.

Thomas stopped.

"You're in your head. You're thinking too much, trying to control every tiny movement." She walked over, adjusted his posture with firm hands on his shoulders. "Feel the floor. Don't think about stepping—feel where your weight wants to go. Your body knows how to move. Trust it."

Thomas closed his eyes. Tried to feel instead of think.

He took a step. Quieter. Not silent, but better.

"Good," Margarethe said, and he heard approval in her voice. "Again."

By the end of the second hour, Thomas could cross the warehouse with only occasional small sounds. Not perfect. Not even close to Margarethe's ghost-like silence. But no longer announcing himself like a town crier.

Margarethe nodded approval. "Better. You learn fast when you stop fighting yourself."

Thomas was breathing hard, sweating despite the cold. "Next lesson?"

She pulled out a simple door lock—old iron, the kind common to half the buildings in Mainz. Set it on the table with two thin pieces of metal. Lockpicks. "Locks."

Thomas stared. "You want me to learn lockpicking?"

"I want you to understand how locks work. The cathedral library will be locked—they always are. I can pick it, but if something happens to me, you need to be able to get in." She demonstrated, sliding the thin metal picks into the mechanism with the ease of long practice. "Feel for the pins. Gentle pressure. Don't force it. Let the lock tell you what it wants. Listen to it."

She popped it open. Made it look effortless, like the lock had opened itself just for her.

"Your turn."

Thomas took the picks. They were awkward in his hands—too thin, too delicate, alien tools for a scribe used to pen and parchment.

He slid them into the lock. Felt... nothing. Just metal scraping metal.

"I don't feel anything," he said.

"You're not listening." Margarethe guided his hands, adjusting the angle of the picks. "The lock is a pattern. Pins arranged in a sequence. Each one has a position it wants to reach. Your job is to find that position. It's not about force—it's about understanding."

Pattern.

The word shifted something in Thomas's mind. Not force—pattern-sense. Like the cipher. Like the dots in the manuscripts. A puzzle waiting to be solved.

He tried again. This time, focusing not on opening the lock but on *listening* to it. Feeling for the pattern.

He felt... something. A resistance in one pin. A tiny amount of give. He adjusted the tension on his pick, felt the resistance shift slightly.

Click.

One pin set.

"There," Margarethe said, surprised. "You felt it."

Thomas's concentration narrowed to the lock and nothing else. The second pin was harder to find. He probed gently, searching for the pattern, feeling for where it wanted to be.

Click.

Third pin. Fourth pin.

The lock opened.

Thomas stared at it, stunned by what he'd just done.

"How did you—" Margarethe took the lock back, examining it like he'd performed some kind of magic. "That took me three weeks to learn. You just did it in ten minutes."

"It's patterns," Thomas said, still breathless from concentration. "Like the cipher. Each pin is a position in a sequence. Once I stopped thinking of it as forcing something and started thinking of it as solving a puzzle..."

Margarethe sat back, studying him with new eyes. Her expression was unreadable. "Wilhelm really did see something in you."

Thomas felt warmth spread through his chest. Different from pride. Deeper than pride. The feeling of being seen, of being valued for something other than his weaknesses.

"Third lesson," Margarethe said, standing abruptly. "Lying."

"What?"

"You need to be able to lie convincingly. If we're caught, if we're questioned, you need to sell a story." She crossed her arms, her face settling into an expression of stern skepticism. "Convince me you're a pilgrim on your way to Cologne. Go."

Thomas tried. Stammered through a story about visiting a shrine to Saint Ursula, praying for his sick mother, needing food and shelter for the journey.

Margarethe's expression was flat. "I'd arrest you immediately. Again."

He tried again. Somehow worse. He could feel his face flushing, his tells broadcasting every lie.

Fifteen minutes later, Margarethe held up a hand. "Stop. Just stop. You're hopeless at this."

"I know," Thomas said miserably.

"No, I mean truly, catastrophically, dangerously hopeless. Your face gives away everything you're thinking. Every lie is written across your forehead like illuminated text." She pinched the bridge of her nose. "Fine. New plan: you don't talk. If we're questioned, you're a mute. Took a vow of silence. Religious devotion. I'll do all the talking."

"I can try to get better—"

"No. Some people are born liars—they slip into falsehoods like water. You're born honest. It's not a flaw in normal life. But in this life, in this moment, it's a liability." She sighed. "Just don't talk unless absolutely necessary. Understood?"

"Understood."

"Last lesson." She pulled out two practice daggers—blunted blades, the edges rounded, safe for training but still heavy enough to hurt. "Defense."

Thomas eyed them warily. "I told you, I'm weak. I can't fight—"

"I'm not teaching you to fight. I'm teaching you not to die immediately when someone tries to kill you." She handed him one blade. "Hold it like this. Blade down, close to your body. It's not a sword—you don't swing it around like some knight in a song. You thrust, you slash close, you create distance and run."

She demonstrated. Fast, economical movements that wasted no energy. Efficient violence.

"Now defend yourself."

She came at him—slow, telegraphed, giving him all the time in the world to react.

Thomas stumbled backward, nearly dropped the blade, tripped over his own feet, and fell hard on his ass. The impact knocked the wind out of him.

Margarethe stood over him, one eyebrow raised.

Then she laughed.

It was the first real laugh Thomas had heard from her—not bitter, not sharp with dark humor, just genuine. It transformed her entire face, softened every hard line, made her look younger and less haunted. For a moment, she wasn't a survivor or a killer or a keeper of dangerous secrets. She was just a woman who found something funny.

"I'm sorry," she said, still laughing, extending a hand to help him up. "I know I shouldn't laugh. But you looked exactly like a startled chicken."

Thomas took her hand, let her pull him to his feet. He was grinning despite his embarrassment. "I warned you I was useless."

"Useless is a strong word. Entertainingly incompetent, maybe." She was still smiling. "Again. And this time, try to stay on your feet for at least three seconds."

They trained for another hour. Thomas didn't improve much—he was simply not built for physical combat, his body too weak from years of scholarly work, his reflexes too slow. But Margarethe taught him a few basics: how to create distance, how to protect his vitals, how to run effectively without tripping over his own robe.

"You're not going to win a fight," she said finally, wiping sweat from her forehead with the back of her hand. "But you might survive one long enough for me to kill whoever's attacking you."

"Reassuring," Thomas said drily.

"It's realistic." She sheathed the practice blades. "You're a scholar, Thomas. Not a warrior. Use your strengths—patterns, locks, thinking three steps ahead. Leave the violence to me."

"What if something happens to you?"

"Then you run. Like I told you." But her voice was softer now, less harsh. She hesitated, then said, "You asked about my scar earlier."

Thomas looked at the thin line along her jaw. He had wondered about it, but hadn't pressed.

"I got it trusting the wrong person," Margarethe said quietly. Her hand drifted unconsciously to one of her daggers—her father's blades. "A merchant who said he was part of the network. Said he could get me into a library the Order had seized, that he knew the guard rotation, the safe times. Turned out he was an informant. Led me straight into a trap."

"How did you escape?"

"My father came for me. Fought three men to get me out." Her voice was tight with old grief. "He took a wound that never fully healed. The blade went deep into his side. Infection set in. He died six months later, in pain, because of my mistake. Because I trusted when I should have been suspicious."

Thomas's chest tightened. "I'm sorry."

"Don't be. It taught me a lesson: trust is expensive, and betrayal is cheap." She met his eyes directly. "I'm telling you this because tonight, in that cathedral, we're going to be trusting a lot. Trusting Oskar's information. Trusting Brother Marcus to let us in. Trusting the locks to open and the Gray Robes not to be waiting in the shadows. That's a dangerous amount of trust."

"Are you saying we shouldn't go?"

"No. I'm saying be ready for it to go wrong. Be ready to run. Be ready to fight if we have to." She pulled her cloak on, checked that her real daggers—sharp blades, killing blades—were secure. "And Thomas?"

"Yes?"

"If something goes wrong tonight—if I tell you to run—you actually do it this time. Don't try to be brave. Don't try to save me. You're carrying your grandfather's legacy. That matters more than my life or yours."

"You matter to me," Thomas said before he could stop himself.

Margarethe froze. For a moment, something flickered across her face—surprise, maybe, or fear. Something vulnerable that she quickly hid.

"Don't," she said quietly. "Don't make me matter. Everyone who matters to me dies. My mother. My father. Every merchant in my father's network who tried to protect me. It's a pattern I can't break."

"Maybe the pattern is wrong," Thomas said. "Maybe you've just been unlucky."

"There's no such thing as luck. Just preparation and consequences." But there was something in her eyes now—not warmth, exactly. But maybe the possibility of it. A crack in the wall. "I'll try not to die, then."

"You're terrible at lying, remember?" But she almost smiled. "Come on. It's almost nightfall. We need to eat something, rest if we can. Midnight comes faster than you think."

She walked past him toward the corner where they'd stored the food.

Thomas stood there a moment longer, feeling the weight of the coming night settle on his shoulders.

Tonight, they would break into the cathedral.

Tonight, they would face the Order on their own ground, in their own sacred space.

And tonight, he would discover if he was truly Wilhelm's grandson—or just another weak boy pretending to be brave.

But for now, in this moment, with Margarethe's rare laughter still echoing in the warehouse and the iron key heavy in his pocket, Thomas allowed himself to hope.

Maybe they could do this.

Maybe they could survive.

Maybe he wasn't useless after all.

---

## Scene 4: The Cathedral Infiltration

Midnight found them in the shadows across from Cathedral St. Martin.

The great church loomed against the night sky like a mountain of stone and faith, its spire a dark needle pointing toward uncaring stars. Somewhere in the city, plague bells rang—another cart of bodies making its slow journey to the mass graves, another list of the dead growing longer. Thomas pulled his cloak tighter against the November cold. His breath came out in visible clouds.

"Remember the plan," Margarethe whispered. "Chapter house entrance on the south side. Psalm 117, verse 1—*Praise the Lord, all nations*. Brother Marcus should let us in. If he doesn't, if something's wrong, we run. No hesitation."

"And if he does?" Thomas asked.

"Then we have one hour. Maybe less. The Gray Robes change guard at Lauds—that's our deadline." She checked the street—empty, silent except for distant coughing from a plague house three doors down. "Stay close. Stay quiet. And for the love of God, don't trip over your own feet."

"I've been practicing," Thomas said with a hint of wounded pride.

"I know. You're almost competent now." But she was smiling in the darkness.

They crossed the square quickly, keeping to the shadows cast by the moon. The cathedral's side entrance was a small wooden door, unremarkable and easy to miss unless you knew it was there. The iron lock was visible even in the dim light—more complex than the practice lock, heavier, newer.

Margarethe knocked. Three soft raps.

Silence.

Thomas's heart hammered hard enough that he was sure it could be heard. This was it. If Oskar had been wrong, if Brother Marcus wasn't sympathetic, if the Order was waiting on the other side of that door—

The door opened a crack.

A young monk peered out, his face pale and nervous in the candlelight he carried. "Yes?"

Margarethe leaned close, pitched her voice just loud enough to be heard. "*Praise the Lord, all nations*," she quoted softly. Psalm 117, verse 1—the code Oskar had given them.

The monk's eyes widened. Recognition and fear in equal measure. He glanced behind him into the darkness of the chapter house, then opened the door wider. "Quickly. Before someone sees."

They slipped inside. The chapter house was dark except for the single candle the monk carried, its flame barely pushing back the shadows. The smell hit Thomas immediately—incense and old stone, beeswax and wood polish. So different from the plague-stink of the streets that it almost made him dizzy. This was a smell of order, of ritual, of a world that still believed in structure when everything outside was falling apart.

"Brother Marcus?" Margarethe asked, though she must have already known.

"Yes." The monk bolted the door behind them—three heavy bolts sliding home with solid clicks. He was young, Thomas realized. Maybe twenty-five, with the kind of face that looked like it wanted to be kind but had learned not to be. "Oskar sent word you'd come. I've been watching for you since Compline."

"The library?" Thomas asked, unable to help himself.

Marcus gestured down a dark corridor with his candle. "Through the nave, past the high altar, behind the choir. The door is locked, but..." He looked at Margarethe's face, seemed to understand. "You'll manage, I imagine."

"Why are you helping us?" Thomas couldn't stop himself from asking. The question had been burning in his mind since Oskar had first mentioned a sympathetic monk.

Marcus's smile was sad, tinged with old grief. "Because Wilhelm of Eltville was kind to me when I was a novice and everyone else treated me like I was invisible. Because knowledge should serve God and humanity, not power and control. Because the Order has forgotten what mercy means." He handed Margarethe the candle, and his hand shook slightly. "One hour. No more. After that, I can't help you. I won't even be able to pretend I didn't know you were here."

"Thank you," Margarethe said quietly.

"Don't thank me until you're safely out." He opened the interior door. "Go. And may God protect you, because I can't."

They went.

The nave of the cathedral was vast and dark, their footsteps echoing despite Thomas's best efforts at silence. Each step seemed to announce itself to the vaulted ceiling, to the painted saints staring down from the walls. Moonlight filtered through stained glass windows, painting the floor in patches of deep red and blue—colors that looked like blood and bruises in the darkness.

Thomas had been in cathedrals before. Had attended services, had helped Brother Hamo deliver manuscripts to cathedral libraries. But never like this. Never as a fugitive. Never in darkness with his life hanging by a thread. Never with his heart hammering so hard it hurt.

They reached the high altar. Behind it, a smaller door—the entrance to the choir and, beyond that, the library where the cathedral kept its treasures.

Locked.

Margarethe pulled out her picks, but Thomas stopped her with a touch on her arm. "Let me try."

She hesitated for just a moment, then nodded. "Fast as you can. We're exposed here."

Thomas knelt before the lock. Inserted the picks with hands that trembled slightly. Felt for the pattern the way Margarethe had taught him.

This lock was more complex than the practice one—six pins instead of four, the tolerances tighter, the springs stiffer. But the principle was the same. Pattern recognition. Understanding the sequence. Listening to what the lock wanted to tell him.

First pin. Second pin. Third—

The third pin stuck, resisting. Thomas adjusted the tension on his pick, probed more gently. Felt for the edge where resistance became give.

*Come on. Show me where you want to be. Tell me the pattern.*

Click.

Fourth pin. Fifth pin. Sixth pin.

The lock opened.

"Forty seconds," Margarethe breathed. He could hear the surprise in her voice even though she kept it low. "You did that in forty seconds."

Thomas stood, his hands shaking from adrenaline and intense concentration. "The pattern was clear once I stopped trying to force it."

"You're going to be insufferable about this, aren't you?" But she was smiling.

They slipped into the library.

And Thomas forgot how to breathe.

The room was enormous—two full stories of manuscripts and knowledge, treasures beyond counting. Chained volumes lined the walls and filled standing desks. Latin, Greek, even some Arabic texts that must have cost a fortune to acquire. Theological works, natural philosophy, astronomy, medicine. The collected wisdom of centuries, preserved and protected behind locked doors.

Or hoarded and controlled, depending on your perspective. Depending on whether you were Wilhelm, who believed knowledge should be free, or the Order, who believed it should be guarded.

"There," Margarethe pointed to a desk near the back, her voice barely a whisper. "The Boethius commentaries. That's where Hamo was working before they killed him."

Thomas crossed to the desk on legs that felt suddenly unsteady. Found the commentary—the same thick volume he'd seen that terrible morning in the scriptorium. Someone had cleaned it carefully, but he could still see faint brown stains on the vellum if he looked closely enough.

Hamo's blood.

He pushed down the grief that rose like bile in his throat. *Later. Grieve later. Right now, finish what he started.*

He pulled out the iron key, studied the manuscript margins. Colored dots, carefully placed with a precision that spoke of years of practice. Red and blue and yellow, encoding information that had cost so many lives. His grandfather's life. Hamo's life. Margarethe's father.

"Which page?" Margarethe asked. She'd moved to watch the door, her hand resting on her dagger.

"I don't... wait." Thomas flipped through carefully, treating the blood-stained vellum with reverent hands. The dots appeared on multiple pages, but one section had a much denser concentration. The commentary on *Consolation of Philosophy*, Book V. The section discussing fate and free will and the nature of divine knowledge.

How grimly appropriate.

He found what looked like a textual anchor—the phrase *"divine foreknowledge"* underlined in faded ink. Aligned the key with careful precision.

Through the aperture, dots appeared in sharp focus. Thomas counted automatically, his mind calculating patterns.

"Five red, two blue, three yellow," he murmured. "Position five-two-three. That's..." He closed his eyes, searching his memory of Margarethe's lessons. "I don't know that code."

"Let me see." Margarethe leaned over his shoulder, close enough that he could smell rosemary and steel. She looked at the pattern and her face went pale. "That's not a location code."

"Then what is it?"

"A warning." She pointed to the specific arrangement with a finger that trembled slightly. "See how the blue dots bracket the red ones in pairs? That's a danger pattern—it means the information itself is dangerous. And the count—five-two-three—that's a date marker. Day, month, year in the Order's calendar system."

"Date of what?"

"I don't know. The cipher usually marks places, not events." She stopped, staring at the page more closely. "Thomas. Look at the text itself. Not the dots. The actual words Hamo was copying."

Thomas read the Latin. Standard theological discussion of divine providence and whether God knew the future or whether free will existed. The eternal question that scholars had debated for centuries.

Except.

In the margin, in tiny script barely visible even in the candlelight, was a notation in different ink. Fresher ink. Hamo's handwriting.

*They know what's coming. They always have. The network didn't predict the plague—they prepared for it. How far back does this pattern go?*

Thomas's hands went cold.

"He figured it out," Margarethe whispered. "Hamo realized the cipher wasn't just recording plague survival patterns. It was—"

"Expecting it." Thomas flipped back through the manuscript with growing urgency. More marginal notes in Hamo's increasingly frantic hand:

*Dots placed fifty years before plague arrival in some regions. Impossible unless they knew it was coming. Unless they planned it.*

*Check Constantinople records. Plague of Justinian, 541 CE. Do the patterns match?*

*If true, then Wilhelm was right. The network is old. Older than the Church. Older than—*

The last entry stopped mid-sentence, the ink trailing off. That must have been when the Gray Robes came for him. When Brother Anselm's blade ended his work forever.

"We need to copy this," Thomas said urgently. "All of it. If Hamo died to understand this, if this is what they killed him for—"

"No time." Margarethe had moved to the door, was listening intently. "We've been here twenty minutes already. We need to find what we came for and leave before—"

Footsteps in the corridor outside.

Multiple sets. Heavy boots, not the soft sandals of monks.

Margarethe's hand went to her dagger in one smooth motion. "Gray Robes."

"Brother Marcus—" Thomas started.

"Either he betrayed us or they caught him. Doesn't matter which." Margarethe looked around the library with quick, desperate eyes. One entrance. No windows large enough for escape. Stone walls too thick to break through. "We're trapped."

The footsteps were getting closer. Calm voices, methodical:

"—check the library first—"

"—Brother Marcus confessed they entered through the chapter house—"

"—Brother Anselm wants them alive if possible, but dead is acceptable—"

"The key," Margarethe hissed. "Use it. Find the next location—now. If we're going to die here, at least understand what we're dying for."

Thomas turned back to the manuscript, hands shaking so badly he could barely hold the key steady. Forced himself to concentrate. *Breathe. Focus. See the pattern.*

The cipher. If Hamo had been working on this commentary when they killed him, there had to be more. Another layer. Another revelation.

Another page. Even denser concentration of dots, arranged in patterns he'd never seen before. He aligned the key, barely breathing.

Seven red, seven blue, seven yellow. Position seven-seven-seven.

"What does that mean?" Thomas asked urgently.

Margarethe's eyes widened as she looked at the pattern. "Seven. That's the sacred number. Seven days of creation, seven cardinal virtues, seven—"

"Seven keys," Thomas breathed, understanding hitting him like cold water. "This isn't encoding a location. It's encoding a revelation. The cipher is telling us something."

"What?"

Thomas stared at the pattern, forcing his grandfather's gift to work. Pattern recognition. See the whole, not just the parts.

He looked at the entire page, not just the dots in isolation. The text, the illuminations around the margins, the structure of how everything fit together.

And he saw it.

The dots weren't just markers for locations or warnings. They formed lines. Connections. A map.

But not a map of places.

A map of people. Of bloodlines. Of families tracked across generations.

"They weren't just recording who survived the plague," Thomas said, his voice shaking. "They were tracking bloodlines. Specific families. Specific traits passed down through generations." He pointed to the genealogical pattern in the dots. "This manuscript is from 1200—nearly 150 years old. The plague came in 1347. They were tracking these families for over a century before the plague arrived. Before it was supposed to arrive."

The footsteps were in the library now. Searching systematically. Getting closer.

"—check between the manuscript stacks—"

"—they have to be here somewhere—"

"—spread out, cover all exits—"

Margarethe grabbed Thomas's arm hard enough to hurt. "We need to go. Right now."

"There's no other way out—"

"Then we make one." She looked up at the library's second floor—a gallery with more manuscripts and rare books. And high on the wall, narrow windows. Too small for a grown man to fit through.

But maybe not too small for a thin scribe.

"Up," Margarethe commanded. "Move!"

They ran for the spiral stairs to the gallery. The Gray Robes saw them—shouts echoed through the library:

"There! The upper level!"

"Cut them off at the stairs!"

Thomas and Margarethe reached the gallery, iron-shod boots pounding up the stairs behind them. The high windows were twenty feet above the ground outside. Thomas could see moonlight through the thick glass.

Margarethe pulled out a dagger, used the pommel to smash the glass. The sound was deafeningly loud—glass shattering, shards falling both inward and outward. An alarm for anyone within a hundred yards.

Shouts below. Multiple voices. Footsteps thundering on the stairs.

"Go!" Margarethe boosted Thomas toward the broken window. Sharp glass edges tore at his clothes, cut his arms, but he was thin enough to fit. Barely. He squeezed through, emerged onto a narrow stone ledge running along the cathedral's exterior.

Two stories up. The cobblestone square far below, hard and unforgiving.

Margarethe followed him through the window with the grace of someone who'd escaped through tight spaces before. A Gray Robe behind her tried to squeeze through, got stuck halfway, cursed.

"Can you climb?" Margarethe asked, already scanning the wall.

Thomas looked up at the rough-cut stone, the carved decorations, the gargoyles leering from the eaves with their grotesque faces. "I have no choice, do I?"

"Not really."

Behind them, the Gray Robe was pulling himself back inside, shouting orders: "Get archers! Now!"

Thomas started climbing.

His hands found holds in the carved stone—decorative elements that suddenly became survival tools. His arms protested immediately, muscles burning with effort. His fingers scraped against rough stone, tore on sharp edges. One foot slipped, and for a terrible moment he hung by his fingers alone, two stories of empty air below him, death waiting on the cobblestones.

"Don't look down," Margarethe called. She climbed beside him with easy grace, making it look effortless. "Focus on the next handhold. One at a time. You're doing fine."

Thomas didn't feel fine. He felt like he was about to die. But he climbed.

His world narrowed to stone and breath and burning muscles. Reach. Grip. Pull. Find the next hold. Don't think about falling. Don't think about the drop. Just climb.

He reached the roof edge. Pulled himself over with the last of his strength, lay gasping on the cold tiles. His arms felt like they were on fire.

Margarethe landed beside him, barely winded. "We need to keep moving. They'll have archers if—"

An arrow struck the tiles three inches from Thomas's head, the impact sending chips of ceramic flying.

"—if they think to post them," Margarethe finished. "Move!"

They ran.

The cathedral roof was a landscape of slopes and spires, gargoyles and gutters, treacherous tiles that cracked and slid under their feet. Thomas ran without thinking, driven by pure terror. Below, more shouts. The Gray Robes were gathering, spreading around the building, blocking the ways out.

"There!" Margarethe pointed to an adjacent building—a wealthy merchant's house, its roof close enough to the cathedral that the gap between them was maybe six feet.

She didn't hesitate. Ran and jumped the gap in one smooth motion, landing in a crouch on the other roof.

Thomas stopped at the edge. Looked at the distance. Six feet might as well have been a mile. The gap between the roofs opened like a mouth waiting to swallow him.

"Thomas!" Margarethe called. "Jump! Now!"

More arrows whistled past. Behind him, a Gray Robe was pulling himself onto the roof, sword in hand.

Thomas backed up three steps. Ran. Leaped into the void.

For a moment, he was flying. Suspended over empty space. The ground was so far below. The wind was so cold. Time stretched and slowed and he was certain he wouldn't make it—

Then Margarethe's hand caught his wrist, yanking him forward with strength he didn't know she had. He crashed onto the merchant's roof tiles hard enough to knock the wind out of him.

"I hate you," Thomas gasped.

"You love me," she shot back, grinning wildly despite everything. "Now move before they start shooting at us again!"
